<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Global catastrophic risk</h1>			<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
				<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>				<div id="contentSub"></div>
				<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Hypothetical future event that has the potential to damage human well-being on a global scale</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Global catastrophic risks" redirects here. For the book, see <a href="/wiki/Global_Catastrophic_Risks_(book)" title="Global Catastrophic Risks (book)">Global Catastrophic Risks (book)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Impact_event.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Impact_event.jpg/300px-Impact_event.jpg" width="300" height="209" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Impact_event.jpg/450px-Impact_event.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cb/Impact_event.jpg 2x" data-file-width="517" data-file-height="360" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Impact_event.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist's impression of a major <a href="/wiki/Impact_event" title="Impact event">asteroid impact</a>. An asteroid with an impact strength of a billion atomic bombs may have caused the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">extinction of the dinosaurs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Schulte10_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schulte10-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>A <b>global catastrophic risk</b> is a hypothetical future event which could damage human well-being on a global scale,<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> even crippling or destroying <a href="/wiki/Modernity" title="Modernity">modern civilization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-world_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-world-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> An event that could cause <a href="/wiki/Human_extinction" title="Human extinction">human extinction</a> or permanently and drastically curtail humanity's potential is known as an <b>existential risk</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-types_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-types-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Potential global catastrophic risks include anthropogenic risks, caused by humans (technology, governance, climate change), and natural or external risks.<sup id="cite_ref-world_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-world-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Examples of technology risks are <a href="/wiki/Existential_risk_from_advanced_artificial_intelligence" class="mw-redirect" title="Existential risk from advanced artificial intelligence">hostile artificial intelligence</a> and destructive <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnology</a> or <a href="/wiki/Molecular_nanotechnology#Risks" title="Molecular nanotechnology">nanotechnology</a>. Insufficient or malign <a href="/wiki/Global_governance" title="Global governance">global governance</a> creates risks in the social and political domain, such as a <a href="/wiki/World_War_III" title="World War III">global war</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_holocaust" title="Nuclear holocaust">nuclear holocaust</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bioterrorism" title="Bioterrorism">bioterrorism</a> using <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_organism" title="Genetically modified organism">genetically modified organisms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyberterrorism" title="Cyberterrorism">cyberterrorism</a> destroying <a href="/wiki/Critical_infrastructure" title="Critical infrastructure">critical infrastructure</a> like the <a href="/wiki/Electrical_grid" title="Electrical grid">electrical grid</a>; or the failure to manage a natural <a href="/wiki/Pandemic" title="Pandemic">pandemic</a>. Problems and risks in the domain of <a href="/wiki/Earth_system_governance" title="Earth system governance">earth system governance</a> include <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Environmental_degradation" title="Environmental degradation">environmental degradation</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinction</a> of species, <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a> as a result of <a href="/wiki/Social_development_theory" title="Social development theory">non-equitable</a> resource distribution, <a href="/wiki/Human_overpopulation" title="Human overpopulation">human overpopulation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crop_failures" class="mw-redirect" title="Crop failures">crop failures</a> and non-<a href="/wiki/Sustainable_agriculture" title="Sustainable agriculture">sustainable agriculture</a>. Examples of non-anthropogenic risks are an asteroid <a href="/wiki/Impact_event" title="Impact event">impact event</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Supervolcano" title="Supervolcano">supervolcanic</a> <a href="/wiki/Types_of_volcanic_eruptions" title="Types of volcanic eruptions">eruption</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Gamma-ray_burst#Hypothetical_effects_on_Earth_in_the_past" title="Gamma-ray burst">lethal gamma-ray burst</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Geomagnetic_storm" title="Geomagnetic storm">geomagnetic storm</a> destroying electronic equipment, natural long-term <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>, or hostile <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">extraterrestrial life</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classifications">Classifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_catastrophic_risk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Classifications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a href="/wiki/File:X-risk-chart-en-01a.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/X-risk-chart-en-01a.svg/500px-X-risk-chart-en-01a.svg.png" width="500" height="489" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/X-risk-chart-en-01a.svg/750px-X-risk-chart-en-01a.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/X-risk-chart-en-01a.svg/1000px-X-risk-chart-en-01a.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="587" data-file-height="574" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:X-risk-chart-en-01a.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Scope/intensity grid from Bostrom's paper "Existential Risk Prevention as Global Priority"<sup id="cite_ref-priority_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-priority-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_catastrophic_vs._existential">Global catastrophic vs. existential</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_catastrophic_risk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Global catastrophic vs. existential">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Philosopher <a href="/wiki/Nick_Bostrom" title="Nick Bostrom">Nick Bostrom</a> classifies risks according to their scope and intensity.<sup id="cite_ref-priority_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-priority-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> A "global catastrophic risk" is any risk that is at least "global" in scope, and is not subjectively "imperceptible" in intensity. Those that are at least "trans-generational" (affecting all future generations) in scope and "terminal"<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="does &quot;terminal&quot; mean human extinction or not? (April 2017)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> in intensity are classified as existential risks. While a global catastrophic risk may kill the vast majority of life on earth, humanity could still potentially recover. An existential risk, on the other hand, is one that either destroys humanity (and, presumably, all but the most rudimentary species of <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">non-human lifeforms</a> and/or <a href="/wiki/Plants" class="mw-redirect" title="Plants">plant life</a>) entirely or at least prevents any chance of civilization recovering. Bostrom considers existential risks to be far more significant.<sup id="cite_ref-waste_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-waste-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Similarly, in <i><a href="/wiki/Catastrophe:_Risk_and_Response" title="Catastrophe: Risk and Response">Catastrophe: Risk and Response</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Posner" title="Richard Posner">Richard Posner</a> singles out and groups together events that bring about "utter overthrow or ruin" on a global, rather than a "local or regional" scale. Posner singles out such events as worthy of special attention on <a href="/wiki/Cost-benefit" class="mw-redirect" title="Cost-benefit">cost-benefit</a> grounds because they could directly or indirectly jeopardize the survival of the human race as a whole.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Posner's events include <a href="/wiki/Impact_event#Frequency_and_Risk" title="Impact event">meteor impacts</a>, runaway <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grey_goo" title="Grey goo">grey goo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bioterrorism" title="Bioterrorism">bioterrorism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Safety_of_high_energy_particle_collision_experiments" class="mw-redirect" title="Safety of high energy particle collision experiments">particle accelerator accidents</a>.
</p><p>Researchers experience difficulty in studying near human extinction directly, since humanity has never been destroyed before.<sup id="cite_ref-observation_selection_effects_cirkovic_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-observation_selection_effects_cirkovic-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> While this does not mean that it will not be in the future, it does make modelling existential risks difficult, due in part to <a href="/wiki/Survivorship_bias" title="Survivorship bias">survivorship bias</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_classifications">Other classifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_catastrophic_risk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Other classifications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Bostrom identifies four types of existential risk. "Bangs" are sudden catastrophes, which may be accidental or deliberate. He thinks the most likely sources of bangs are malicious use of <a href="/wiki/Nanotechnology" title="Nanotechnology">nanotechnology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_warfare" title="Nuclear warfare">nuclear war</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Simulation_hypothesis" title="Simulation hypothesis">the possibility that the universe is a simulation</a> that will end. "Crunches" are scenarios in which humanity survives but civilization is slowly destroyed. The most likely causes of this, he believes, are <a href="/wiki/Environmental_degradation" title="Environmental degradation">exhaustion of natural resources</a>, a stable <a href="/wiki/World_government" title="World government">global government</a> that prevents technological progress, or <a href="/wiki/Dysgenics" title="Dysgenics">dysgenic pressures</a> that lower average intelligence. "Shrieks" are undesirable futures. For example, if a single mind enhances its powers by merging with a computer, it could dominate human civilization. Bostrom believes that this scenario is most likely, followed by flawed superintelligence and a repressive totalitarian regime. "Whimpers" are the gradual decline of human civilization or current values. He thinks the most likely cause would be evolution changing moral preference, followed by extraterrestrial invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-types_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-types-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Likelihood">Likelihood</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_catastrophic_risk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Likelihood">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Some risks, such as that from asteroid impact, with a one-in-a-million chance of causing humanity's extinction in the next century,<sup id="cite_ref-Matheny_xrisk_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Matheny_xrisk-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> have had their probabilities predicted using straightforward, well-understood, and (in principle) precise methods (although even in cases like these, the exact rate of large impacts is contested).<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, the frequency of volcanic eruptions of sufficient magnitude to cause catastrophic climate change, similar to the <a href="/wiki/Toba_catastrophe_theory" title="Toba catastrophe theory">Toba Eruption</a>, which may have almost caused the extinction of the human race,<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> has been estimated at about 1 in every 50,000 years.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The relative danger posed by other threats is much more difficult to calculate. Given the limitations of ordinary calculation and modeling, expert elicitation is frequently used instead to obtain probability estimates.<sup id="cite_ref-probabilities_and_methodologies_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-probabilities_and_methodologies-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> In 2008, an informal survey of experts on different global catastrophic risks at the Global Catastrophic Risk Conference at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">University of Oxford</a> suggested a 19% chance of human extinction by the year 2100. The conference report cautions that the results should be taken "with a grain of salt".<sup id="cite_ref-:0_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<dl><dd><dl><dd><table class="wikitable">

<tbody><tr>

</td></tr>

</td></tr>

</td></tr>

</td></tr>

</td></tr></tbody></table></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<dl><dd><dl><dd><small>Table source: <a href="/wiki/Future_of_Humanity_Institute" title="Future of Humanity Institute">Future of Humanity Institute</a>, 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup></small></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<p>The 2016 annual report by the <a href="/wiki/Global_Challenges_Foundation" title="Global Challenges Foundation">Global Challenges Foundation</a> estimates that an average American is more than five times more likely to die during a human-extinction event than in a car crash.<sup id="cite_ref-gcf_atlantic_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gcf_atlantic-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gcf_home_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gcf_home-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are significant methodological challenges in estimating these risks with precision. Most attention has been given to risks to human civilization over the next 100 years, but forecasting for this length of time is difficult. The types of threats posed by nature may prove relatively constant, though new risks could be discovered. Anthropogenic threats, however, are likely to change dramatically with the development of new technology; while volcanoes have been a threat throughout history, nuclear weapons have only been an issue since the 20th century. Historically, the ability of experts to predict the future over these timescales has proved very limited. Man-made threats such as nuclear war or <a href="/wiki/Nanotechnology" title="Nanotechnology">nanotechnology</a> are harder to predict than natural threats, due to the inherent methodological difficulties in the social sciences. In general, it is hard to estimate the magnitude of the risk from this or other dangers, especially as both international relations and technology can change rapidly.
</p><p>Existential risks pose unique challenges to prediction, even more than other long-term events, because of <a href="/wiki/Anthropic_principle" title="Anthropic principle">observation selection effects</a>. Unlike with most events, the failure of a complete <a href="/wiki/Extinction_event" title="Extinction event">extinction event</a> to occur in the past is not evidence against their likelihood in the future, because every world that has experienced such an extinction event has no observers, so regardless of their frequency, no civilization observes existential risks in its history.<sup id="cite_ref-observation_selection_effects_cirkovic_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-observation_selection_effects_cirkovic-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> These <a href="/wiki/Anthropic_principle" title="Anthropic principle">anthropic</a> issues can be avoided by looking at evidence that does not have such selection effects, such as asteroid impact craters on the Moon, or directly evaluating the likely impact of new technology.<sup id="cite_ref-priority_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-priority-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to known and tangible risks, unforeseeable <a href="/wiki/Black_swan_theory" title="Black swan theory">black swan</a> extinction events may occur, presenting an additional methodological problem.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Moral_importance_of_existential_risk">Moral importance of existential risk</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_catastrophic_risk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Moral importance of existential risk">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Some scholars have strongly favored reducing existential risk on the grounds that it greatly benefits future generations. <a href="/wiki/Derek_Parfit" title="Derek Parfit">Derek Parfit</a> argues that extinction would be a great loss because our descendants could potentially survive for four billion years before the <a href="/wiki/Sun#After_core_hydrogen_exhaustion" title="Sun">expansion of the Sun</a> makes the Earth uninhabitable.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nick_Bostrom" title="Nick Bostrom">Nick Bostrom</a> argues that there is even greater potential in colonizing space. If future humans colonize space, they may be able to support a very large number of people on other planets, potentially lasting for trillions of years.<sup id="cite_ref-waste_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-waste-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Therefore, reducing existential risk by even a small amount would have a very significant impact on the expected number of people who will exist in the future.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Exponential_discounting" title="Exponential discounting">Exponential discounting</a> might make these future benefits much less significant. However, Jason Matheny has argued that such discounting is inappropriate when assessing the value of existential risk reduction.<sup id="cite_ref-Matheny_xrisk_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Matheny_xrisk-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some economists have discussed the importance of global catastrophic risks, though not existential risks. Martin Weitzman argues that most of the expected economic damage from climate change may come from the small chance that warming greatly exceeds the mid-range expectations, resulting in catastrophic damage.<sup id="cite_ref-weitzman1_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weitzman1-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Richard_Posner" title="Richard Posner">Richard Posner</a> has argued that we are doing far too little, in general, about small, hard-to-estimate risks of large-scale catastrophes.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Numerous <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_biases" class="mw-redirect" title="Cognitive biases">cognitive biases</a> can influence people's judgment of the importance of existential risks, including <a href="/wiki/Scope_insensitivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Scope insensitivity">scope insensitivity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hyperbolic_discounting" title="Hyperbolic discounting">hyperbolic discounting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Availability_heuristic" title="Availability heuristic">availability heuristic</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Conjunction_fallacy" title="Conjunction fallacy">conjunction fallacy</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Affect_heuristic" title="Affect heuristic">affect heuristic</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Overconfidence_effect" title="Overconfidence effect">overconfidence effect</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Biases_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Biases-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Scope insensitivity influences how bad people consider the extinction of the human race to be. For example, when people are motivated to donate money to altruistic causes, the quantity they are willing to give does not increase linearly with the magnitude of the issue: people are roughly as concerned about 200,000 birds getting stuck in oil as they are about 2,000.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, people are often more concerned about threats to individuals than to larger groups.<sup id="cite_ref-Biases_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Biases-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are economic reasons that can explain why so little effort is going into existential risk reduction. It is a global good, so even if a large nation decreases it, that nation will only enjoy a small fraction of the benefit of doing so. Furthermore, the vast majority of the benefits may be enjoyed by far future generations, and though these quadrillions of future people would in theory perhaps be willing to pay massive sums for existential risk reduction, no mechanism for such a transaction exists.<sup id="cite_ref-priority_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-priority-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Potential_sources_of_risk">Potential sources of risk</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_catastrophic_risk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Potential sources of risk">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Some sources of catastrophic risk are natural, such as meteor impacts or supervolcanoes. Some of these have caused mass extinctions in the past. On the other hand, some risks are man-made, such as global warming,<sup id="cite_ref-IPCC_AR5_attribution_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_AR5_attribution-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> environmental degradation, engineered pandemics and nuclear war. 
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anthropogenic">Anthropogenic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_catastrophic_risk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Anthropogenic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Cambridge Project at <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Cambridge University">Cambridge University</a> states that the "greatest threats" to the human species are man-made; they are artificial intelligence, global warming, nuclear war, and rogue biotechnology.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Future_of_Humanity_Institute" title="Future of Humanity Institute">Future of Humanity Institute</a> also states that human extinction is more likely to result from anthropogenic causes than natural causes.<sup id="cite_ref-priority_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-priority-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Frequently_Asked_Questions_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frequently_Asked_Questions-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Artificial_intelligence">Artificial intelligence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_catastrophic_risk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Artificial intelligence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Existential_risk_from_advanced_artificial_intelligence" class="mw-redirect" title="Existential risk from advanced artificial intelligence">Existential risk from advanced artificial intelligence</a>, <a href="/wiki/AI_takeover" title="AI takeover">AI takeover</a>, <a href="/wiki/Friendly_artificial_intelligence" title="Friendly artificial intelligence">Friendly artificial intelligence</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Technological_singularity" title="Technological singularity">Technological singularity</a></div>
<p>It has been suggested that <a href="/wiki/Ethics_of_artificial_intelligence#Unintended_consequences" title="Ethics of artificial intelligence">learning computers</a> that rapidly become <a href="/wiki/Superintelligence" title="Superintelligence">superintelligent</a> may take unforeseen actions, or that <a href="/wiki/Robot#Social_impact" title="Robot">robots</a> would out-compete humanity (one <a href="/wiki/Technological_singularity" title="Technological singularity">technological singularity</a> scenario).<sup id="cite_ref-billjoy_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-billjoy-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Because of its exceptional scheduling and organizational capability and the range of novel technologies it could develop, it is possible that the first Earth superintelligence to emerge could rapidly become matchless and unrivaled: conceivably it would be able to bring about almost any possible outcome, and be able to foil virtually any attempt that threatened to prevent it achieving its objectives.<sup id="cite_ref-Bostrom_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bostrom-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> It could eliminate, wiping out if it chose, any other challenging rival intellects; alternatively it might manipulate or persuade them to change their behavior towards its own interests, or it may merely obstruct their attempts at interference.<sup id="cite_ref-Bostrom_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bostrom-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> In Bostrom's book, <i><a href="/wiki/Superintelligence:_Paths,_Dangers,_Strategies" title="Superintelligence: Paths, Dangers, Strategies">Superintelligence: Paths, Dangers, Strategies</a></i>, he defines this as the control problem.<sup id="cite_ref-Bostrom2_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bostrom2-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Physicist <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Hawking" title="Stephen Hawking">Stephen Hawking</a>, <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> founder <a href="/wiki/Bill_Gates" title="Bill Gates">Bill Gates</a> and <a href="/wiki/SpaceX" title="SpaceX">SpaceX</a> founder <a href="/wiki/Elon_Musk" title="Elon Musk">Elon Musk</a> have echoed these concerns, with Hawking theorizing that this could "spell the end of the human race".<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>In 2009, the <a href="/wiki/Association_for_the_Advancement_of_Artificial_Intelligence" title="Association for the Advancement of Artificial Intelligence">Association for the Advancement of Artificial Intelligence</a> (AAAI) hosted a conference to discuss whether computers and robots might be able to acquire any sort of <a href="/wiki/Autonomy" title="Autonomy">autonomy</a>, and how much these abilities might pose a threat or hazard. They noted that some robots have acquired various forms of semi-autonomy, including being able to find power sources on their own and being able to independently choose targets to attack with weapons. They also noted that some computer viruses can evade elimination and have achieved "cockroach intelligence." They noted that self-awareness as depicted in science-fiction is probably unlikely, but that there were other potential hazards and pitfalls.<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes_july09_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes_july09-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Various media sources and scientific groups have noted separate trends in differing areas which might together result in greater robotic functionalities and autonomy, and which pose some inherent concerns.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>A survey of AI experts estimated that the chance of human-level machine learning having an "extremely bad (e.g., human extinction)" long-term effect on humanity is 5%.<sup id="cite_ref-AI_survey_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AI_survey-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> A survey by the Future of Humanity Institute estimated a 5% probability of extinction by superintelligence by 2100.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eliezer_Yudkowsky" title="Eliezer Yudkowsky">Eliezer Yudkowsky</a> believes that risks from artificial intelligence are harder to predict than any other known risks due to bias from <a href="/wiki/Anthropomorphism" title="Anthropomorphism">anthropomorphism</a>. Since people base their judgments of artificial intelligence on their own experience, he claims that they underestimate the potential power of AI.<sup id="cite_ref-Yudkowsky_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yudkowsky-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Biotechnology">Biotechnology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_catastrophic_risk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Biotechnology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology_risk" title="Biotechnology risk">Biotechnology risk</a></div>
<p>Biotechnology can pose a global catastrophic risk in the form of bioengineered organisms (<a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fungi" class="mw-redirect" title="Fungi">fungi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a> or <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animals</a>). In many cases the organism will be a <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogen</a> of humans, <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a>, crops or other organisms we depend upon (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Pollinator" title="Pollinator">pollinators</a> or <a href="/wiki/Gut_flora" title="Gut flora">gut bacteria</a>). However, any organism able to catastrophically disrupt <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_function" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecosystem function">ecosystem functions</a>, e.g. highly competitive <a href="/wiki/Weed" title="Weed">weeds</a>, outcompeting essential crops, poses a biotechnology risk.
</p><p>A biotechnology catastrophe may be caused by accidentally releasing a genetically engineered organism escaping from controlled environments, by the planned release of such an organism which then turns out to have unforeseen and catastrophic interactions with essential natural or agro-ecosystems, or by intentional usage of <a href="/wiki/Biological_agent" title="Biological agent">biological agents</a> in <a href="/wiki/Biological_warfare" title="Biological warfare">biological warfare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bioterrorism" title="Bioterrorism">bioterrorism</a> attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-GCRs_bio-chapter_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCRs_bio-chapter-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Pathogens may be intentionally or unintentionally genetically modified to change virulence and other characteristics.<sup id="cite_ref-GCRs_bio-chapter_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCRs_bio-chapter-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> For example, a group of Australian researchers unintentionally changed characteristics of the <a href="/wiki/Mousepox" class="mw-redirect" title="Mousepox">mousepox</a> virus while trying to develop a virus to sterilize rodents.<sup id="cite_ref-GCRs_bio-chapter_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCRs_bio-chapter-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> The modified virus became highly lethal even in vaccinated and naturally resistant mice.<sup id="cite_ref-Sandberg_xrisk_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sandberg_xrisk-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mousepoxstudy_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mousepoxstudy-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The technological means to genetically modify virus characteristics are likely to become more widely available in the future if not properly regulated.<sup id="cite_ref-GCRs_bio-chapter_36-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCRs_bio-chapter-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Terrorism" title="Terrorism">Terrorist</a> applications of biotechnology have historically been infrequent. To what extent this is due to a lack of capabilities or motivation is not resolved.<sup id="cite_ref-GCRs_bio-chapter_36-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCRs_bio-chapter-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> However, given current development, more risk from novel, engineered pathogens is to be expected in the future.<sup id="cite_ref-GCRs_bio-chapter_36-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCRs_bio-chapter-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Exponential growth has been observed in the <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnology</a> sector, and Noun and Chyba predict that this will lead to major increases in biotechnological capabilities in the coming decades.<sup id="cite_ref-GCRs_bio-chapter_36-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCRs_bio-chapter-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> They argue that risks from <a href="/wiki/Biological_warfare" title="Biological warfare">biological warfare</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bioterrorism" title="Bioterrorism">bioterrorism</a> are distinct from nuclear and chemical threats because biological pathogens are easier to mass-produce and their production is hard to control (especially as the technological capabilities are becoming available even to individual users).<sup id="cite_ref-GCRs_bio-chapter_36-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCRs_bio-chapter-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> A survey by the Future of Humanity Institute estimated a 2% probability of extinction from engineered pandemics by 2100.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Noun and Chyba propose three categories of measures to reduce risks from biotechnology and natural pandemics: Regulation or prevention of potentially dangerous research, improved recognition of outbreaks and developing facilities to mitigate disease outbreaks (e.g. better and/or more widely distributed vaccines).<sup id="cite_ref-GCRs_bio-chapter_36-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCRs_bio-chapter-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cyberattack">Cyberattack</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_catastrophic_risk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Cyberattack">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cyberattack" title="Cyberattack">Cyberattack</a></div>
<p>Cyberattacks have the potential to destroy everything from personal data to electric grids. <a href="/wiki/Christine_Peterson" title="Christine Peterson">Christine Peterson</a>, co-founder and past president of the <a href="/wiki/Foresight_Institute" title="Foresight Institute">Foresight Institute</a>, believes a cyberattack on electric grids has the potential to be a catastrophic risk.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_warming">Global warming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_catastrophic_risk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Global warming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_global_warming" title="Effects of global warming">Effects of global warming</a> and <a href="/wiki/Runaway_climate_change" title="Runaway climate change">Runaway climate change</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">Global warming</a> refers to the warming caused by human technology since the 19th century or earlier. Projections of future climate change suggest further global warming, <a href="/wiki/Sea_level_rise" title="Sea level rise">sea level rise</a>, and an increase in the frequency and severity of some extreme weather events and weather-related disasters. Effects of global warming include <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_loss" title="Biodiversity loss">loss of biodiversity</a>, stresses to existing food-producing systems, increased spread of known infectious diseases such as malaria, and rapid mutation of <a href="/wiki/Microorganisms" class="mw-redirect" title="Microorganisms">microorganisms</a>. In November 2017, a statement by 15,364 scientists from 184 countries indicated that increasing levels of greenhouse gases from use of fossil fuels, human population growth, deforestation, and overuse of land for agricultural production, particularly by farming <a href="/wiki/Ruminant" title="Ruminant">ruminants</a> for meat consumption, are trending in ways that forecast an increase in human misery over coming decades.<sup id="cite_ref-world_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-world-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_disaster">Environmental disaster</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_catastrophic_risk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Environmental disaster">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_disaster" title="Environmental disaster">Environmental disaster</a></div>
<p>An environmental or ecological disaster, such as world <a href="/wiki/Crop_failure" class="mw-redirect" title="Crop failure">crop failure</a> and collapse of <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_services" title="Ecosystem services">ecosystem services</a>, could be induced by the present trends of <a href="/wiki/Human_overpopulation" title="Human overpopulation">overpopulation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Economic_development" title="Economic development">economic development</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Chiarelli1998_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chiarelli1998-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> and non-<a href="/wiki/Sustainable_agriculture" title="Sustainable agriculture">sustainable agriculture</a>. Most environmental scenarios involve one or more of the following: <a href="/wiki/Holocene_extinction_event" class="mw-redirect" title="Holocene extinction event">Holocene extinction event</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Water_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="Water crisis">scarcity of water</a> that could lead to approximately one half of the Earth's population being without safe drinking water, <a href="/wiki/Pollinator_decline" title="Pollinator decline">pollinator decline</a>, <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">overfishing</a>, massive <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Desertification" title="Desertification">desertification</a>, <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>, or massive <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">water pollution</a> episodes. Detected in the early 21st century, a threat in this direction is <a href="/wiki/Colony_collapse_disorder" title="Colony collapse disorder">colony collapse disorder</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> a phenomenon that might foreshadow the imminent extinction<sup id="cite_ref-Lovgren_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lovgren-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> of the <a href="/wiki/Western_honeybee" class="mw-redirect" title="Western honeybee">Western honeybee</a>. As the bee plays a vital role in pollination, its extinction would severely disrupt the <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">food chain</a>.
</p><p>An October 2017 report published in <a href="/wiki/The_Lancet" title="The Lancet">The Lancet</a> stated that toxic air, <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">water</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soil_pollution" class="mw-redirect" title="Soil pollution">soils</a>, and workplaces were collectively responsible for 9 million deaths worldwide in 2015, particularly from <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a> which was linked to deaths by increasing susceptibility to non-infectious diseases, such as <a href="/wiki/Heart_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Heart disease">heart disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">stroke</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lung_cancer" title="Lung cancer">lung cancer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-carrington_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-carrington-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> The report warned that the pollution crisis was exceeding "the envelope on the amount of pollution the Earth can carry" and “threatens the continuing survival of human societies”.<sup id="cite_ref-carrington_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-carrington-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mineral_resource_exhaustion">Mineral resource exhaustion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_catastrophic_risk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Mineral resource exhaustion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Romanian_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Romanian American">Romanian American</a> economist <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Georgescu-Roegen" title="Nicholas Georgescu-Roegen">Nicholas Georgescu-Roegen</a>, a <a href="/wiki/List_of_people_considered_father_or_mother_of_a_scientific_field" title="List of people considered father or mother of a scientific field">progenitor</a> in <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Paradigm_shift#Kuhnian_paradigm_shifts" title="Paradigm shift">paradigm founder</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ecological_economics" title="Ecological economics">ecological economics</a>, has argued that the <a href="/wiki/Sustainability#Carrying_capacity" title="Sustainability">carrying capacity</a> of Earth — that is, Earth's capacity to sustain human populations and consumption levels — is bound to decrease sometime in the future as Earth's finite stock of <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">mineral resources</a> is presently being extracted and put to use; and consequently, that the <a href="/wiki/World_economy" title="World economy">world economy</a> as a whole is heading towards an <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Georgescu-Roegen#Population_pressure,_mineral_resource_exhaustion_and_the_end_of_mankind" title="Nicholas Georgescu-Roegen">inevitable future collapse, leading to the demise of human civilization itself</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ngr01_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ngr01-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>303f</span></sup> Ecological economist and <a href="/wiki/Steady-state_economy#Herman_Daly&#39;s_concept_of_a_steady-state_economy" title="Steady-state economy">steady-state theorist</a> <a href="/wiki/Herman_Daly" title="Herman Daly">Herman Daly</a>, a student of Georgescu-Roegen, has <a href="/wiki/Steady-state_economy#Daly&#39;s_credo" title="Steady-state economy">propounded the same argument</a> by asserting that "... all we can do is to avoid wasting the limited capacity of <a href="/wiki/Genesis_creation_narrative" title="Genesis creation narrative">creation</a> to support present and future life [on Earth]."<sup id="cite_ref-hd01_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hd01-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>370</span></sup>
</p><p>Ever since Georgescu-Roegen and Daly published these views, various scholars in the field have been discussing the existential impossibility of allocating earth's finite stock of mineral resources evenly among an unknown number of present and future generations. This number of generations is likely to remain unknown to us, as there is no way — or only little way — of knowing in advance if or when <a href="/wiki/Human_extinction" title="Human extinction">mankind will ultimately face extinction</a>. In effect, <i>any</i> conceivable intertemporal allocation of the stock will inevitably end up with universal economic decline at some future point.<sup id="cite_ref-jr01_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jr01-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>253–256</span></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-kb01_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kb01-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>165</span></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-jm01_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jm01-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>168–171</span></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-jg01_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jg01-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>150–153</span></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-js01_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-js01-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>106–109</span></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-ck01_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ck01-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>546–549</span></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-ap01_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ap01-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>142–145</span></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Experimental_technology_accident">Experimental technology accident</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_catastrophic_risk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Experimental technology accident">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Grey_goo" title="Grey goo">Grey goo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bioterrorism" title="Bioterrorism">Bioterrorism</a></div>
<p>Nick Bostrom suggested that in the pursuit of knowledge, humanity might inadvertently create a device that could destroy Earth and the Solar System.<sup id="cite_ref-physicsaccident_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-physicsaccident-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Investigations in nuclear and high-energy physics could create unusual conditions with catastrophic consequences. For example, scientists worried that the <a href="/wiki/Trinity_(nuclear_test)#Test_predictions" title="Trinity (nuclear test)">first nuclear test</a> might ignite the atmosphere.<sup id="cite_ref-Hamming_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hamming-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-LA602_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LA602-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> More recently, others <a href="/wiki/Relativistic_Heavy_Ion_Collider#Critics_of_high-energy_experiments" title="Relativistic Heavy Ion Collider">worried that the RHIC</a><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> or the <a href="/wiki/Safety_of_particle_collisions_at_the_Large_Hadron_Collider" class="mw-redirect" title="Safety of particle collisions at the Large Hadron Collider">Large Hadron Collider</a> might start a chain-reaction global disaster involving black holes, strangelets, or false vacuum states. These particular concerns have been refuted,<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> but the general concern remains.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">Biotechnology</a> could lead to the creation of a <a href="/wiki/Pandemic" title="Pandemic">pandemic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chemical_warfare" title="Chemical warfare">chemical warfare</a> could be taken to an extreme, <a href="/wiki/Nanotechnology" title="Nanotechnology">nanotechnology</a> could lead to <a href="/wiki/Grey_goo" title="Grey goo">grey goo</a> in which out-of-control <a href="/wiki/Self_replication" class="mw-redirect" title="Self replication">self-replicating</a> robots consume all living matter on earth while building more of themselves—in both cases, either deliberately or by accident.<sup id="cite_ref-drexler_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-drexler-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nanotechnology">Nanotechnology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_catastrophic_risk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Nanotechnology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Grey_goo" title="Grey goo">Grey goo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potential_risks_of_nanotechnology" class="mw-redirect" title="Potential risks of nanotechnology">Potential risks of nanotechnology</a></div>
<p>Many nanoscale technologies are in development or currently in use.<sup id="cite_ref-GCRs_nano-chapter_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCRs_nano-chapter-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> The only one that appears to pose a significant global catastrophic risk is <a href="/wiki/Molecular_manufacturing" class="mw-redirect" title="Molecular manufacturing">molecular manufacturing</a>, a technique that would make it possible to build complex structures at atomic precision.<sup id="cite_ref-Foresight_nano_FAQ_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Foresight_nano_FAQ-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Molecular manufacturing requires significant advances in nanotechnology, but once achieved could produce highly advanced products at low costs and in large quantities in nanofactories of desktop proportions.<sup id="cite_ref-GCRs_nano-chapter_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCRs_nano-chapter-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Foresight_nano_FAQ_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Foresight_nano_FAQ-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> When nanofactories gain the ability to produce other nanofactories, production may only be limited by relatively abundant factors such as input materials, energy and software.<sup id="cite_ref-GCRs_nano-chapter_63-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCRs_nano-chapter-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Molecular manufacturing could be used to cheaply produce, among many other products, highly advanced, durable weapons.<sup id="cite_ref-GCRs_nano-chapter_63-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCRs_nano-chapter-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Being equipped with compact computers and motors these could be increasingly autonomous and have a large range of capabilities.<sup id="cite_ref-GCRs_nano-chapter_63-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCRs_nano-chapter-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Chris_Phoenix_(nanotechnologist)" title="Chris Phoenix (nanotechnologist)">Chris Phoenix</a> and Treder classify catastrophic risks posed by nanotechnology into three categories:
</p>
<ol><li>From augmenting the development of other technologies such as AI and biotechnology.</li>
<li>By enabling mass-production of potentially dangerous products that cause risk dynamics (such as arms races) depending on how they are used.</li>
<li>From uncontrolled self-perpetuating processes with destructive effects.</li></ol>
<p>Several researchers state that the bulk of risk from nanotechnology comes from the potential to lead to war, arms races and destructive global government.<sup id="cite_ref-Sandberg_xrisk_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sandberg_xrisk-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GCRs_nano-chapter_63-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCRs_nano-chapter-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Several reasons have been suggested why the availability of nanotech weaponry may with significant likelihood lead to unstable arms races (compared to e.g. nuclear arms races):
</p>
<ol><li>A large number of players may be tempted to enter the race since the threshold for doing so is low;<sup id="cite_ref-GCRs_nano-chapter_63-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCRs_nano-chapter-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The ability to make weapons with molecular manufacturing will be cheap and easy to hide;<sup id="cite_ref-GCRs_nano-chapter_63-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCRs_nano-chapter-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Therefore, lack of insight into the other parties' capabilities can tempt players to arm out of caution or to launch preemptive strikes;<sup id="cite_ref-GCRs_nano-chapter_63-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCRs_nano-chapter-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Molecular manufacturing may reduce dependency on international trade,<sup id="cite_ref-GCRs_nano-chapter_63-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCRs_nano-chapter-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> a potential peace-promoting factor;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wars_of_aggression" class="mw-redirect" title="Wars of aggression">Wars of aggression</a> may pose a smaller economic threat to the aggressor since manufacturing is cheap and humans may not be needed on the battlefield.<sup id="cite_ref-GCRs_nano-chapter_63-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCRs_nano-chapter-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup></li></ol>
<p>Since self-regulation by all state and non-state actors seems hard to achieve,<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> measures to mitigate war-related risks have mainly been proposed in the area of <a href="/wiki/Multilateralism" title="Multilateralism">international cooperation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-GCRs_nano-chapter_63-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCRs_nano-chapter-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-crnano_int_control_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crnano_int_control-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> International infrastructure may be expanded giving more sovereignty to the international level. This could help coordinate efforts for arms control. International institutions dedicated specifically to nanotechnology (perhaps analogously to the International Atomic Energy Agency <a href="/wiki/IAEA" class="mw-redirect" title="IAEA">IAEA</a>) or general arms control may also be designed.<sup id="cite_ref-crnano_int_control_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crnano_int_control-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> One may also jointly make <a href="/wiki/Differential_technological_development" title="Differential technological development">differential technological progress</a> on defensive technologies, a policy that players should usually favour.<sup id="cite_ref-GCRs_nano-chapter_63-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCRs_nano-chapter-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> The Center for Responsible Nanotechnology also suggests some technical restrictions.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Improved transparency regarding technological capabilities may be another important facilitator for arms-control.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Grey_goo" title="Grey goo">Grey goo</a> is another catastrophic scenario, which was proposed by <a href="/wiki/K._Eric_Drexler" title="K. Eric Drexler">Eric Drexler</a> in his 1986 book <i><a href="/wiki/Engines_of_Creation" title="Engines of Creation">Engines of Creation</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> and has been a theme in mainstream media and fiction.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> This scenario involves tiny <a href="/wiki/Self-replicating_machine" title="Self-replicating machine">self-replicating robots</a> that consume the entire biosphere using it as a source of energy and building blocks. Nowadays, however, nanotech experts—including Drexler—discredit the scenario. According to Phoenix, a "so-called grey goo could only be the product of a deliberate and difficult engineering process, not an accident".<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Warfare_and_mass_destruction">Warfare and mass destruction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_catastrophic_risk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Warfare and mass destruction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_holocaust" title="Nuclear holocaust">Nuclear holocaust</a>, <a href="/wiki/World_War_III" title="World War III">World War III</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cold_War_II" title="Cold War II">Cold War II</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Liberty-shall-not-perish-Pennell.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Liberty-shall-not-perish-Pennell.jpeg/190px-Liberty-shall-not-perish-Pennell.jpeg" width="190" height="263" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Liberty-shall-not-perish-Pennell.jpeg/285px-Liberty-shall-not-perish-Pennell.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Liberty-shall-not-perish-Pennell.jpeg/380px-Liberty-shall-not-perish-Pennell.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="7040" data-file-height="9728" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Liberty-shall-not-perish-Pennell.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Joseph_Pennell" title="Joseph Pennell">Joseph Pennell</a>'s 1918 <a href="/wiki/Liberty_bond" title="Liberty bond">Liberty bond</a> poster calls up the pictorial image of an invaded, burning <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The scenarios that have been explored most frequently are nuclear warfare and <a href="/wiki/Doomsday_device" title="Doomsday device">doomsday devices</a>. Although the probability of a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_war" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear war">nuclear war</a> per year is slim, Professor <a href="/wiki/Martin_Hellman" title="Martin Hellman">Martin Hellman</a> has described it as inevitable in the long run; unless the probability approaches zero, inevitably there will come a day when civilization's luck runs out.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> During the Cuban missile crisis, U.S. president <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> estimated the odds of nuclear war at "somewhere between one out of three and even".<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The United States and Russia have a combined <a href="/wiki/Arsenal" title="Arsenal">arsenal</a> of 14,700 <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear weapons">nuclear weapons</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FASNukes_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FASNukes-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> and there is an estimated total of 15,700 nuclear weapons in existence worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-FASNukes_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FASNukes-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Beyond nuclear, other military threats to humanity include <a href="/wiki/Biological_warfare" title="Biological warfare">biological warfare</a> (BW). By contrast, <a href="/wiki/Chemical_warfare" title="Chemical warfare">chemical warfare</a>, while able to create multiple local catastrophes, is unlikely to create a global one.
</p><p>Nuclear war could yield unprecedented human <a href="/wiki/Death_tolls" class="mw-redirect" title="Death tolls">death tolls</a> and <a href="/wiki/Habitat_destruction" title="Habitat destruction">habitat destruction</a>. Detonating large numbers of nuclear weapons would have an immediate, short term and long-term effects on the climate, causing <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_winter" title="Nuclear winter">cold weather</a> and reduced sunlight and <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> that may generate significant upheaval in advanced civilizations.<sup id="cite_ref-nuclearwinter_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nuclearwinter-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> However, while popular perception sometimes takes nuclear war as "the end of the world", experts assign low probability to human extinction from nuclear war.<sup id="cite_ref-Martin1982_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martin1982-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Shulman2012_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shulman2012-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> In 1982, <a href="/wiki/Brian_Martin_(social_scientist)" title="Brian Martin (social scientist)">Brian Martin</a> estimated that a US–Soviet nuclear exchange might kill 400–450 million directly, mostly in the United States, Europe and Russia, and maybe several hundred million more through follow-up consequences in those same areas.<sup id="cite_ref-Martin1982_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martin1982-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> A survey by the Future of Humanity Institute estimated a 4% probability of extinction from warfare by 2100, with a 1% chance of extinction from nuclear warfare.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_14-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="World_population_and_agricultural_crisis">World population and agricultural crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_catastrophic_risk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: World population and agricultural crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Malthusian_catastrophe" title="Malthusian catastrophe">Malthusian catastrophe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Human_overpopulation" title="Human overpopulation">Human overpopulation</a></div>
<p>The 20th century saw a rapid increase in <a href="/wiki/Human_population" class="mw-redirect" title="Human population">human population</a> due to <a href="/wiki/History_of_medicine#Modern_medicine" title="History of medicine">medical developments</a> and massive increases in agricultural productivity<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> such as the <a href="/wiki/Green_Revolution" title="Green Revolution">Green Revolution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Between 1950 and 1984, as the Green Revolution transformed agriculture around the globe, world grain production increased by 250%. The Green Revolution in agriculture helped food production to keep pace with worldwide <a href="/wiki/Population_growth" title="Population growth">population growth</a> or actually enabled population growth. The energy for the Green Revolution was provided by fossil fuels in the form of fertilizers (natural gas), pesticides (oil), and <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbon</a>-fueled <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> David Pimentel, professor of ecology and agriculture at <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell University</a>, and Mario Giampietro, senior researcher at the National Research Institute on Food and Nutrition (INRAN), place in their 1994 study <i>Food, Land, Population and the U.S. Economy</i> the maximum U.S. population for a <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">sustainable economy</a> at 200 million. To achieve a sustainable economy and avert disaster, the United States must reduce its population by at least one-third, and world population will have to be reduced by two-thirds, says the study.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The authors of this study believe that the mentioned agricultural crisis will begin to have an effect on the world after 2020, and will become critical after 2050. Geologist <a href="/wiki/Dale_Allen_Pfeiffer" title="Dale Allen Pfeiffer">Dale Allen Pfeiffer</a> claims that coming decades could see spiraling food prices without relief and massive starvation on a global level such as never experienced before.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Wheat is humanity's third-most-produced cereal. Extant fungal infections such as <a href="/wiki/Ug99" title="Ug99">Ug99</a><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> (a kind of <a href="/wiki/Stem_rust" title="Stem rust">stem rust</a>) can cause 100% crop losses in most modern varieties. Little or no treatment is possible and infection spreads on the wind. Should the world's large grain-producing areas become infected, the ensuing crisis in wheat availability would lead to price spikes and shortages in other food products.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-anthropogenic">Non-anthropogenic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_catastrophic_risk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Non-anthropogenic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Asteroid_impact">Asteroid impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_catastrophic_risk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Asteroid impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Asteroid-impact_avoidance" class="mw-redirect" title="Asteroid-impact avoidance">Asteroid-impact avoidance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Impact_event" title="Impact event">Impact event</a></div>
<p>Several asteroids have collided with earth in recent geological history. The <a href="/wiki/Chicxulub_crater" title="Chicxulub crater">Chicxulub asteroid</a>, for example, is theorized to have caused the extinction of the non-avian dinosaurs 66 million years ago at the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">end of the Cretaceous</a>. No sufficiently large asteroid currently exists in an Earth-crossing orbit; however, a comet of sufficient size to cause human extinction could impact the Earth, though the annual probability may be less than 10<sup>−8</sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-comets_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-comets-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Geoscientist <a href="/wiki/Brian_Toon" class="mw-redirect" title="Brian Toon">Brian Toon</a> estimates that a 60-mile meteorite would be large enough to "incinerate everybody".<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Asteroids with around a 1&#160;km diameter have impacted the Earth on average once every 500,000 years; these are probably too small to pose an extinction risk, but might kill billions of people.<sup id="cite_ref-comets_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-comets-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-meteor_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-meteor-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Larger asteroids are less common. Small <a href="/wiki/Near-Earth_asteroid" class="mw-redirect" title="Near-Earth asteroid">near-Earth asteroids</a> are regularly observed and can impact anywhere on the Earth injuring local populations <sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>. As of 2013, <a href="/wiki/Spaceguard" title="Spaceguard">Spaceguard</a> estimates it has identified 95% of all <a href="/wiki/Near_earth_object" class="mw-redirect" title="Near earth object">NEOs</a> over 1&#160;km in size.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In April 2018, the <a href="/wiki/B612_Foundation" title="B612 Foundation">B612 Foundation</a> reported "It's a 100 per cent certain we'll be hit [by a devastating asteroid], but we're not 100 per cent sure when."<sup id="cite_ref-DS-20180428_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DS-20180428-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-INQ-20180428_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-INQ-20180428-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> In June 2018, the US <a href="/wiki/National_Science_and_Technology_Council" title="National Science and Technology Council">National Science and Technology Council</a> warned that America is unprepared for an asteroid impact event, and has developed and released the <i>"<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.whitehouse.gov/wp-content/uploads/2018/06/National-Near-Earth-Object-Preparedness-Strategy-and-Action-Plan-23-pages-1MB.pdf">National Near-Earth Object Preparedness Strategy Action Plan</a>"</i> to better prepare.<sup id="cite_ref-WH-20180621_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WH-20180621-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GIZ-20180621_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GIZ-20180621-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ICARUS-220180522_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICARUS-220180522-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYT-20180614_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20180614-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYT-20180614c_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20180614c-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Extraterrestrial_invasion">Extraterrestrial invasion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_catastrophic_risk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Extraterrestrial invasion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Alien_invasion" title="Alien invasion">Alien invasion</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">Extraterrestrial life</a> could invade Earth<sup id="cite_ref-aliens_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aliens-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> either to exterminate and supplant human life, enslave it under a colonial system, steal the planet's resources, or destroy the planet altogether.
</p><p>Although evidence of alien life has never been documented, scientists such as <a href="/wiki/Carl_Sagan" title="Carl Sagan">Carl Sagan</a> have postulated that the existence of extraterrestrial life is very likely. In 1969, the "<a href="/wiki/Extra-Terrestrial_Exposure_Law" title="Extra-Terrestrial Exposure Law">Extra-Terrestrial Exposure Law</a>" was added to the United States <a href="/wiki/Code_of_Federal_Regulations" title="Code of Federal Regulations">Code of Federal Regulations</a> (Title 14, Section 1211) in response to the possibility of biological contamination resulting from the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">U.S. Apollo Space Program</a>. It was removed in 1991.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Scientists consider such a scenario technically possible, but unlikely.<sup id="cite_ref-aliensunlikely_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aliensunlikely-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>An article in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> discussed the possible threats for humanity of intentionally sending messages aimed at extraterrestrial life into the cosmos in the context of the <a href="/wiki/Search_for_extraterrestrial_intelligence" title="Search for extraterrestrial intelligence">SETI</a> efforts. Several renowned public figures such as Stephen Hawking and Elon Musk have argued against sending such messages on the grounds that extraterrestrial civilizations with technology are probably far more advanced than humanity and could pose an existential threat to humanity.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_climate_change">Natural climate change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_catastrophic_risk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Natural climate change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Global_catastrophic_risk&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span>  <small><i>(April 2018)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">Climate change</a> refers to a lasting change in the Earth's climate. The climate has ranged from ice ages to warmer periods when palm trees grew in Antarctica. It has been hypothesized that there was also a period called "<a href="/wiki/Snowball_Earth" title="Snowball Earth">snowball Earth</a>" when all the oceans were covered in a layer of ice. These global climatic changes occurred slowly, prior to the rise of human civilization about 10 thousand years ago near the end of the last <a href="/wiki/Ice_Age#Major_Ice_Ages" class="mw-redirect" title="Ice Age">Major Ice Age</a> when the climate became more stable. However, <a href="/wiki/Abrupt_climate_change" title="Abrupt climate change">abrupt climate change</a> on the decade time scale has occurred regionally. Since civilization originated during a period of stable climate, a natural variation into a new climate regime (colder or hotter) could pose a threat to civilization.
</p><p>In the history of the Earth, many <a href="/wiki/Ice_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Ice Age">ice ages</a> are known to have occurred. More ice ages will be possible at an interval of 40,000–100,000 years. An ice age would have a serious impact on civilization because vast areas of land (mainly in North America, Europe, and Asia) could become uninhabitable. It would still be possible to live in the tropical regions, but with possible loss of humidity and water. Currently, the world is in an <a href="/wiki/Interglacial" title="Interglacial">interglacial period</a> within a much older glacial event. The last glacial expansion ended about 10,000 years ago, and all civilizations evolved later than this. Scientists do not predict that a natural ice age will occur anytime soon. This may be due to manmade emissions potentially delaying the possible onset or another ice age for at least another 50,000 years.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cosmic_threats">Cosmic threats</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_catastrophic_risk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Cosmic threats">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A number of astronomical threats have been identified. Massive objects, e.g. a star, large planet or <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black hole</a>, could be catastrophic if a close encounter occurred in the Solar System.
