In April 2008, it was announced that two simulations of long-term planetary movement, one at the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Observatory" title="Paris Observatory">Paris Observatory</a> and the other at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Santa_Cruz" title="University of California, Santa Cruz">University of California, Santa Cruz</a>, indicate a 1% chance that <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>'s orbit could be made unstable by Jupiter's gravitational pull sometime during the lifespan of the Sun. Were this to happen, the simulations suggest a collision with Earth could be one of four possible outcomes (the others being Mercury colliding with the Sun, colliding with Venus, or being ejected from the Solar System altogether). If Mercury were to collide with Earth, all life on Earth could be obliterated entirely: an asteroid 15&#160;km wide is believed to have caused the extinction of the non-avian dinosaurs, whereas Mercury is 4,879&#160;km in diameter.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another cosmic threat is a <a href="/wiki/Gamma-ray_burst#Rate_of_occurrence_and_potential_effects_on_life_on_Earth" title="Gamma-ray burst">gamma-ray burst</a>, typically produced by a <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernova</a> when a star collapses inward on itself and then "bounces" outward in a massive explosion. Under certain circumstances, these events are thought to produce massive bursts of gamma radiation emanating outward from the axis of rotation of the star. If such an event were to occur oriented towards the Earth, the massive amounts of gamma radiation could significantly affect the Earth's atmosphere and pose an existential threat to all life. Such a gamma-ray burst may have been the cause of the <a href="/wiki/Ordovician%E2%80%93Silurian_extinction_events" title="Ordovician–Silurian extinction events">Ordovician–Silurian extinction events</a>. Neither this scenario nor the destabilization of Mercury's orbit are likely in the foreseeable future.<sup id="cite_ref-unlikely_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unlikely-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>If the Solar System were to pass through a <a href="/wiki/Dark_nebula" title="Dark nebula">dark nebula</a>, a cloud of <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">cosmic dust</a>, severe global climate change would occur.<sup id="cite_ref-Galacticdust_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Galacticdust-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A powerful <a href="/wiki/Solar_flare" title="Solar flare">solar flare</a> or solar superstorm, which is a drastic and unusual decrease or increase in the Sun's power output, could have severe consequences for life on Earth.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>If our universe lies within a <a href="/wiki/False_vacuum" title="False vacuum">false vacuum</a>, a bubble of lower-energy vacuum could come to exist by chance or otherwise in our universe, and catalyze the conversion of our universe to a lower energy state in a volume expanding at nearly the speed of light, destroying all that we know without forewarning.<sup id="cite_ref-colemandeluccia_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-colemandeluccia-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag needs further explanation. (July 2015)">further explanation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Such an occurrence is called <a href="/wiki/False_vacuum#Vacuum_decay" title="False vacuum">vacuum decay</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Geomagnetic_reversal">Geomagnetic reversal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_catastrophic_risk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Geomagnetic reversal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geomagnetic_reversal" title="Geomagnetic reversal">Geomagnetic reversal</a></div>
<p>The magnetic poles of the Earth shifted many times in geologic history. The duration of such a shift is still debated. Theories exist that during such times, the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth&#39;s magnetic field">Earth's magnetic field</a> would be substantially weakened, threatening civilization by allowing radiation from the Sun, especially <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_flare" title="Solar flare">solar flares</a> or <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_radiation" class="mw-redirect" title="Cosmic radiation">cosmic radiation</a>, to reach the surface. These theories have been somewhat discredited, as statistical analysis shows no evidence for a correlation between past reversals and past extinctions.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Glassmeier2010_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glassmeier2010-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_pandemic">Global pandemic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_catastrophic_risk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Global pandemic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pandemic" title="Pandemic">Pandemic</a></div>
<p>Numerous historical examples of pandemics<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> had a devastating effect on a large number of people. The present, unprecedented scale and speed of human movement make it more difficult than ever to contain an epidemic through local <a href="/wiki/Quarantine" title="Quarantine">quarantines</a>. A global pandemic has become a realistic threat to human civilization.
</p><p>Naturally <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolving</a> pathogens will ultimately develop an upper limit to their virulence.<sup id="cite_ref-Frank_SA_1996_37–78_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frank_SA_1996_37–78-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Pathogens with the highest virulence, quickly killing their hosts reduce their chances of spread the infection to new hosts or carriers.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> This simple model predicts that - if <a href="/wiki/Virulence" title="Virulence">virulence</a> and transmission are not genetically linked - pathogens will evolve towards low virulence and rapid transmission. However, this is not necessarily a safeguard against a global catastrophe, for the following reasons:
</p><p>1. The fitness advantage of limited virulence is primarily a function of a limited number of hosts. Any pathogen with a high virulence, high transmission rate and long incubation time may have already caused a catastrophic pandemic before ultimately virulence is limited through natural selection.
2. In models where virulence level and rate of transmission are related, high levels of virulence can evolve.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Virulence is instead limited by the existence of complex populations of hosts with different susceptibilities to infection, or by some hosts being geographically isolated.<sup id="cite_ref-Frank_SA_1996_37–78_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frank_SA_1996_37–78-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> The size of the host population and competition between different strains of pathogens can also alter virulence.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> 
3. A pathogen that infects humans as a secondary host and primarily infects another species (a <a href="/wiki/Zoonosis" title="Zoonosis">zoonosis</a>) has no constraints on its virulence in people, since the accidental secondary infections do not affect its evolution.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Volcanism">Volcanism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_catastrophic_risk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Volcanism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Supervolcano" title="Supervolcano">Supervolcano</a></div>
<p>A geological event such as massive <a href="/wiki/Flood_basalt" title="Flood basalt">flood basalt</a>, volcanism, or the eruption of a supervolcano<sup id="cite_ref-supervolcano_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-supervolcano-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> could lead to a so-called volcanic winter, similar to a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_winter" title="Nuclear winter">nuclear winter</a>. One such event, the <a href="/wiki/Toba_eruption" class="mw-redirect" title="Toba eruption">Toba eruption</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Tobaeruption_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tobaeruption-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> occurred in Indonesia about 71,500 years ago. According to the <a href="/wiki/Toba_catastrophe_theory" title="Toba catastrophe theory">Toba catastrophe theory</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Tobacatastrophetheory_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tobacatastrophetheory-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> the event may have reduced human populations to only a few tens of thousands of individuals. <a href="/wiki/Yellowstone_Caldera" title="Yellowstone Caldera">Yellowstone Caldera</a> is another such supervolcano, having undergone 142 or more <a href="/wiki/Caldera" title="Caldera">caldera</a>-forming eruptions in the past 17 million years.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
A massive volcano eruption would eject extraordinary volumes of volcanic dust, toxic and greenhouse gases into the atmosphere with serious effects on global climate (towards extreme global cooling:  <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_winter" title="Volcanic winter">volcanic winter</a> if short-term, and ice age if long-term) or <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> (if greenhouse gases were to prevail).
</p><p>When the supervolcano at <a href="/wiki/Yellowstone_National_Park" title="Yellowstone National Park">Yellowstone</a> last erupted 640,000 years ago, the thinnest layers of the ash ejected from the <a href="/wiki/Yellowstone_Caldera" title="Yellowstone Caldera">caldera</a> spread over most of the United States west of the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a> and part of northeastern Mexico. The <a href="/wiki/Magma" title="Magma">magma</a> covered much of what is now Yellowstone National Park and extended beyond, covering much of the ground from Yellowstone River in the east to the Idaho falls in the west, with some of the flows extending north beyond Mammoth Springs.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to a recent study, if the Yellowstone caldera erupted again as a supervolcano, an ash layer one to three millimeters thick could be deposited as far away as New York, enough to "reduce traction on roads and runways, short out electrical transformers and cause respiratory problems". There would be centimeters of thickness over much of the U.S. Midwest, enough to disrupt crops and livestock, especially if it happened at a critical time in the growing season. The worst-affected city would likely be <a href="/wiki/Billings,_Montana" title="Billings, Montana">Billings, Montana</a>, population 109,000, which the model predicted would be covered with ash estimated as 1.03 to 1.8 meters thick.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The main long-term effect is through global climate change, which reduces the temperature globally by about 5–15 degrees C for a decade, together with the direct effects of the deposits of ash on their crops. A large supervolcano like Toba would deposit one or two meters thickness of ash over an area of several million square kilometers.(1000 cubic kilometers is equivalent to a one-meter thickness of ash spread over a million square kilometers). If that happened in some densely populated agricultural area, such as India, it could destroy one or two seasons of crops for two billion people.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>However, Yellowstone shows no signs of a supereruption at present, and it is not certain that a future supereruption will occur there.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Research published in 2011 finds evidence that massive volcanic eruptions caused massive coal combustion, supporting models for significant generation of greenhouse gases. Researchers have suggested that massive volcanic eruptions through coal beds in Siberia would generate significant greenhouse gases and cause a <a href="/wiki/Runaway_greenhouse_effect" title="Runaway greenhouse effect">runaway greenhouse effect</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Massive eruptions can also throw enough pyroclastic debris and other material into the atmosphere to partially block out the sun and cause a <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_winter" title="Volcanic winter">volcanic winter</a>, as happened on a smaller scale in 1816 following the eruption of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Tambora" title="Mount Tambora">Mount Tambora</a>, the so-called <a href="/wiki/Year_Without_a_Summer" title="Year Without a Summer">Year Without a Summer</a>. Such an eruption might cause the immediate deaths of millions of people several hundred miles from the eruption, and perhaps billions of deaths<sup id="cite_ref-Supervolcano_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Supervolcano-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> worldwide, due to the failure of the <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>, resulting in major crop failures causing starvation on a profound scale.<sup id="cite_ref-Supervolcano_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Supervolcano-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A much more speculative concept is the <a href="/wiki/Verneshot" title="Verneshot">verneshot</a>: a hypothetical volcanic eruption caused by the buildup of gas deep underneath a <a href="/wiki/Craton" title="Craton">craton</a>. Such an event may be forceful enough to launch an extreme amount of material from the <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">crust</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a> into a <a href="/wiki/Sub-orbital" class="mw-redirect" title="Sub-orbital">sub-orbital</a> trajectory.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposed_mitigation">Proposed mitigation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_catastrophic_risk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Proposed mitigation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Planetary_management" title="Planetary management">Planetary management</a> and respecting <a href="/wiki/Planetary_boundaries" title="Planetary boundaries">planetary boundaries</a> have been proposed as approaches to preventing ecological catastrophes. Within the scope of these approaches, the field of <a href="/wiki/Climate_engineering" title="Climate engineering">geoengineering</a> encompasses the deliberate large-scale engineering and manipulation of the planetary environment to combat or counteract anthropogenic changes in atmospheric chemistry. <a href="/wiki/Space_colonization" title="Space colonization">Space colonization</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Space_and_survival" title="Space and survival">proposed alternative</a> to improve the odds of surviving an extinction scenario.<sup id="cite_ref-physorg20100809_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-physorg20100809-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Solutions of this scope may require <a href="/wiki/Megascale_engineering" title="Megascale engineering">megascale engineering</a>.
<a href="/wiki/Food_storage" title="Food storage">Food storage</a> has been proposed globally, but the monetary cost would be high. Furthermore, it would likely contribute to the current millions of deaths per year due to <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Some <a href="/wiki/Survivalist" class="mw-redirect" title="Survivalist">survivalists</a> stock <a href="/wiki/Retreat_(survivalism)" title="Retreat (survivalism)">survival retreats</a> with multiple-year food supplies.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Svalbard_Global_Seed_Vault" title="Svalbard Global Seed Vault">Svalbard Global Seed Vault</a> is buried 400 feet (120&#160;m) inside a mountain on an island in the <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a>. It is designed to hold 2.5 billion seeds from more than 100 countries as a precaution to preserve the world's crops. The surrounding rock is −6&#160;°C (21&#160;°F) (as of 2015) but the vault is kept at −18&#160;°C (0&#160;°F) by refrigerators powered by locally sourced coal.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>More speculatively, if society continues to function and if the biosphere remains habitable, calorie needs for the present human population might in theory be met during an extended absence of sunlight, given sufficient advance planning. Conjectured solutions include growing mushrooms on the dead plant biomass left in the wake of the catastrophe, converting cellulose to sugar, or feeding natural gas to methane-digesting bacteria.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_catastrophic_risks_and_global_governance">Global catastrophic risks and global governance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_catastrophic_risk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Global catastrophic risks and global governance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Insufficient <a href="/wiki/Global_governance" title="Global governance">global governance</a> creates risks in the social and political domain, but the governance mechanisms develop more slowly than technological and social change. There are concerns from governments, the private sector, as well as the general public about the lack of governance mechanisms to efficiently deal with risks, negotiate and adjudicate between diverse and conflicting interests. This is further underlined by an understanding of the interconnectedness of global systemic risks.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organizations">Organizations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_catastrophic_risk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Organizations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bulletin_of_the_Atomic_Scientists" title="Bulletin of the Atomic Scientists">Bulletin of the Atomic Scientists</a> (est. 1945) is one of the oldest global risk organizations, founded after the public became alarmed by the potential of atomic warfare in the aftermath of WWII. It studies risks associated with nuclear war and energy and famously maintains the <a href="/wiki/Doomsday_Clock" title="Doomsday Clock">Doomsday Clock</a> established in 1947. The <a href="/wiki/Foresight_Institute" title="Foresight Institute">Foresight Institute</a> (est. 1986) examines the risks of nanotechnology and its benefits. It was one of the earliest organizations to study the unintended consequences of otherwise harmless technology gone haywire at a global scale. It was founded by <a href="/wiki/K._Eric_Drexler" title="K. Eric Drexler">K. Eric Drexler</a> who postulated "<a href="/wiki/Grey_goo" title="Grey goo">grey goo</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Beginning after 2000, a growing number of scientists, philosophers and tech billionaires created organizations devoted to studying global risks both inside and outside of academia.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Independent <a href="/wiki/Non-governmental_organization" title="Non-governmental organization">non-governmental organizations</a> (NGOs) include the <a href="/wiki/Machine_Intelligence_Research_Institute" title="Machine Intelligence Research Institute">Machine Intelligence Research Institute</a> (est. 2000), which aims to reduce the risk of a catastrophe caused by artificial intelligence,<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> with donors including <a href="/wiki/Peter_Thiel" title="Peter Thiel">Peter Thiel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jed_McCaleb" title="Jed McCaleb">Jed McCaleb</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Threat_Initiative" title="Nuclear Threat Initiative">Nuclear Threat Initiative</a> (est. 2001) seeks to reduce global threats from nuclear, biological and chemical threats, and containment of damage after an event.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> It maintains a nuclear material security index.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> The Lifeboat Foundation (est. 2009) funds research into preventing a technological catastrophe.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Most of the research money funds projects at universities.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> The Global Catastrophic Risk Institute (est. 2011) is a think tank for catastrophic risk. It is funded by the NGO Social and Environmental Entrepreneurs. The <a href="/wiki/Global_Challenges_Foundation" title="Global Challenges Foundation">Global Challenges Foundation</a> (est. 2012), based in Stockholm and founded by <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A1szl%C3%B3_Szombatfalvy" title="László Szombatfalvy">Laszlo Szombatfalvy</a>, releases a yearly report on the state of global risks.<sup id="cite_ref-gcf_atlantic_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gcf_atlantic-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gcf_home_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gcf_home-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Future_of_Life_Institute" title="Future of Life Institute">Future of Life Institute</a> (est. 2014) aims to support research and initiatives for safeguarding life considering new technologies and challenges facing humanity.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Elon_Musk" title="Elon Musk">Elon Musk</a> is one of its biggest donors.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>University-based organizations include the <a href="/wiki/Future_of_Humanity_Institute" title="Future of Humanity Institute">Future of Humanity Institute</a> (est. 2005) which researches the questions of humanity's long-term future, particularly existential risk. It was founded by <a href="/wiki/Nick_Bostrom" title="Nick Bostrom">Nick Bostrom</a> and is based at Oxford University. The <a href="/wiki/Centre_for_the_Study_of_Existential_Risk" title="Centre for the Study of Existential Risk">Centre for the Study of Existential Risk</a> (est. 2012) is a Cambridge-based organization which studies four major technological risks: artificial intelligence, biotechnology, global warming and warfare. All are man-made risks, as <a href="/wiki/Huw_Price" title="Huw Price">Huw Price</a> explained to the AFP news agency, "It seems a reasonable prediction that some time in this or the next century intelligence will escape from the constraints of biology". He added that when this happens "we're no longer the smartest things around," and will risk being at the mercy of "machines that are not malicious, but machines whose interests don't include us."<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Hawking" title="Stephen Hawking">Stephen Hawking</a> was an acting adviser. The <a href="/wiki/Millennium_Alliance_for_Humanity_and_the_Biosphere" title="Millennium Alliance for Humanity and the Biosphere">Millennium Alliance for Humanity and the Biosphere</a> is a Stanford University-based organization focusing on many issues related to global catastrophe by bringing together members of academic in the humanities.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> It was founded by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Ehrlich" title="Paul Ehrlich">Paul Ehrlich</a> among others.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> Stanford University also has the <a href="/wiki/Center_for_International_Security_and_Cooperation" title="Center for International Security and Cooperation">Center for International Security and Cooperation</a> focusing on political cooperation to reduce global catastrophic risk.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Other risk assessment groups are based in or are part of governmental organizations. The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO) includes a division called the Global Alert and Response (GAR) which monitors and responds to global epidemic crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> GAR helps member states with training and coordination of response to epidemics.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Agency_for_International_Development" title="United States Agency for International Development">United States Agency for International Development</a> (USAID) has its Emerging Pandemic Threats Program which aims to prevent and contain naturally generated pandemics at their source.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Livermore_National_Laboratory" title="Lawrence Livermore National Laboratory">Lawrence Livermore National Laboratory</a> has a division called the Global Security Principal Directorate which researches on behalf of the government issues such as bio-security and counter-terrorism.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_catastrophic_risk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-125"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-125">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://pubs.usgs.gov/fs/2005/3024/">"Steam Explosions, Earthquakes, and Volcanic Eruptions—What's in Yellowstone's Future?"</a>. <i>USGS Yellowstone Volcanic Observatory</i>. <q>The USGS puts it like this:      “If another large caldera-forming eruption were to occur at Yellowstone, its effects would be worldwide. Thick ash deposits would bury vast areas of the United States, and injection of huge volumes of volcanic gases into the atmosphere could drastically affect global climate. Fortunately, the Yellowstone volcanic system shows no signs that it is headed toward such an eruption. The probability of a large caldera-forming eruption within the next few thousand years is exceedingly low.”</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=USGS+Yellowstone+Volcanic+Observatory&amp;rft.atitle=Steam+Explosions%2C+Earthquakes%2C+and+Volcanic+Eruptions%E2%80%94What%E2%80%99s+in+Yellowstone%E2%80%99s+Future%3F&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fpubs.usgs.gov%2Ffs%2F2005%2F3024%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGlobal+catastrophic+risk" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>
